The Particle 'Fi' in Talaq: Abu Hanifa vs Sahibayn
Dispute on the effect of the particle 'fi' in time containers for divorce.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between Abu Hanifa's and the Sahibayn's positions on the effect of the particle 'fi' in divorce declarations and evaluate the usul reasoning behind each.

Core Idea
The particle 'fi' does not require full encompassment (isti'ab) in Abu Hanifa's view, unlike the Sahibayn.

This lesson delves into a classical Hanafi usul dispute: does mentioning or omitting the particle 'fi' affect the scope of a time expression in a divorce declaration? The Sahibayn treat both as equivalent, requiring full-day encompassment, while Abu Hanifa distinguishes based on the linguistic container function of 'fi'. Understanding this dispute sharpens your ability to analyze how linguistic features influence legal rulings.

Guided Path
1
Identify the linguistic tool
Recognize that 'fi' is a particle of container (zarfiyya). Understand its basic function in Arabic.
The particle 'fi' indicates containment, not necessarily full encompassment.
2
State the dispute
Describe the difference between Sahibayn (both versions imply isti'ab) and Abu Hanifa (mentioning 'fi' removes isti'ab requirement).
Sahibayn: 'anta taliq ghadan' and 'anta taliq fi ghadan' are equal. Abu Hanifa: mentioning 'fi' changes the ruling.
3
Analyze the proof
Understand Abu Hanifa's dalil: 'fi' indicates a container (zarfiyya) and does not require isti'ab by itself.
The container-like meaning of 'fi' gives flexibility to the time expression.
4
Apply the thamarah
Determine the practical consequence: in a case where husband says 'anta taliq fi ghadan' and later claims end of day, Abu Hanifa accepts his claim judicially; Sahibayn do not.
The difference is between judicial (qada') and religious (diyaanatan) acceptance.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : وفى للظرفية ولكنهم اختلفوا في حذفه واثباته في ظرف الزمان فقالا هما سواء وفرق ابو حنيفة رحمه الله بينهما فيما اذا نوى آخر النهار .
ه فى :
اور (فی) ظرفیت کیلئے آتا ہے لیکن اس کے خذف واثبات میں اختلاف ہے یعنی اس کے حذف اور اثبات میں سے کون ساما قبل کے لئے معیار بنے کا تقاضا کرتا ہے اور کون ظرف ہونے کا تقاضا کرتا ہے اس امر میں اختلاف ہے۔
صاحبین فرماتے ہیں کہ دونوں برابر ہیں یعنی دونوں استیعاب کا تقاضا کرتے ہیں ۔ تو اگر کسی نے اپنی بیوی سے کہا انت طالق غداً یا فہ غد) اور پھر اس نے کہا کہ میں نے تو (غدا) سے آخر نھار کی نیت کی تھی تو دیا تھا اس کی تصدیق کی لیکن قضاء تصدیق نہیں کی جائے گی بلکہ دن کے شروع میں طلاق واقع ہو جائے گی ۔ اما . " نہ رحمہ اللہ دونوں صورتوں میں فرق کرتے ہیں چنانچہ وہ فرماتے ہیں کہ اگر (في) کو حذف کیا تو قضاء تصدیق نہیں کی جائے گی لیکن اگر (فی) کو حذف نہ کیا بلکہ ذکر کیا تو قضاء بھی تصدیق کی جائے گی کیونکہ (فی) کا ذکر کرتا امام صاحب کے نزدیک استیعاب کا تقاضا نہیں کرتا۔
His statement: 'And fi is for container, but they differed in its omission and affirmation in a time container. They (Sahibayn) said both are equal. And Abu Hanifa differentiated between them when he intended the end of the day.'
As for fi: And 'fi' is for container, but there is disagreement in its omission and affirmation, i.e., which of its omission and affirmation necessitates it being a standard for the earlier part and which necessitates it being a container. The Sahibayn say both are equal, i.e., both necessitate encompassment. So if someone said to his wife 'You are divorced tomorrow' or 'You are divorced fi (in) tomorrow', and then he said 'I intended by tomorrow the end of the day', his statement is attested concerning religious matters but not in judicial ruling; rather divorce occurs at the beginning of the day. But Abu Hanifa distinguishes between the two cases. He says: if 'fi' is omitted, his statement is not attested in judicial ruling, but if 'fi' is not omitted but rather mentioned, then his statement is also attested in judicial ruling, because mentioning 'fi', according to Imam, does not necessitate encompassment.
Source pages p. 202

Commentary
This passage presents a dispute in Hanafi fiqh between Abu Hanifa and the two disciples (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani) regarding the particle 'fi' when used with a time expression in declarations of divorce. The Sahibayn hold that mentioning or omitting 'fi' (like saying 'anta taliq ghadan' vs 'anta taliq fi ghadan') both imply that the entire day (isti'ab) is intended; thus if the husband later claims he only meant the end of the day, his claim is accepted religiously but not judicially, so divorce takes effect at the start of the day. Abu Hanifa distinguishes: if 'fi' is omitted, the same ruling applies; but if 'fi' is mentioned, the word 'fi' indicates container, not necessarily full encompassment, so the husband's claim that he intended the end of the day is accepted even judicially. This shows how the meaning of a particle affects legal outcomes.

Key Terms
فی (fi): A particle indicating container (zarfiyya), which may or may not imply full encompassment.
استیعاب (isti'ab): Full encompassment or inclusion of the entire time or space.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the difference between Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn when someone says 'anta taliq fi ghadan' and later claims he intended the end of the day?
A) All agree his claim is accepted judicially
B) Sahibayn accept his claim, Abu Hanifa does not
C) Abu Hanifa accepts his claim judicially, Sahibayn only religiously
D) None accept his claim

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi usul, the particle 'fi' does not require full encompassment, affecting judicial acceptance of a husband's intention in talaq.

Interpreting 'Kayfa' in Talaq and Itq: Abu Hanifa vs. Sahibayn
Ikhtilaf on interpretation of 'kayfa' in talaq and itq

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi position on 'kayfa' in divorce and manumission, and evaluate the disagreement between Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn on the basis of usul principles.

Core Idea
In Hanafi usul, 'kayfa' is considered لغو when indicating state is impossible; this literal reading clashes with Sahibayn's contextual approach.

This lesson examines a classic usul dispute: when a husband says 'You are divorced how you wish' (anti taliq kayfa shi'ta), what exactly falls? Abu Hanifa holds that 'kayfa' is redundant, yielding one revocable divorce immediately with the wife's option conditional on intent. The Sahibayn argue that state and description are inseparable from the original, giving the wife full control. This disagreement reveals deeper principles about linguistic interpretation and qiyas in non-sensible legal matters.

Guided Path
1
Identify the disputed phrase and its linguistic issue
The phrase 'anti taliq kayfa shi'ta' contains 'kayfa', which normally asks about state. Abu Hanifa argues that if the state question does not make sense in context (since the wife cannot 'wish' a state she is not yet in), the word becomes invalid (لاغو).
The Urdu commentary explains: 'if the meaning of asking is not correct, then the word kayfa becomes invalid.'
2
State Abu Hanifa's ruling and reasoning
For manumission ('anta hurr kayfa shi'ta'), immediate freedom occurs. For divorce, one revocable divorce occurs immediately. The remainder in description (ba'inah) and number (two or three) is left to the wife, conditional upon the husband's intention. If the husband has no intention, only one revocable divorce falls.
This is based on the original linguistic meaning of 'kayfa' and qiyas.
3
Present the Sahibayn's opposing view
Abu Yusuf and Muhammad argue that in non-sensible legal matters like divorce and manumission, the state and description are equivalent to the original. Therefore, both the original (divorce/freedom) and its description should be tied to the wife's wish to avoid preference without preponderance (tarjih bila murajjih).
They rely on the principle that in non-sensible matters, the state and original are alike.
4
Analyze Abu Hanifa's response
Abu Hanifa responds that the Sahibayn's approach entails making the original follow the description, which is contrary to qiyas (analogy). According to qiyas, the original should not be subservient to a subordinate description.
This response shows the Hanafi commitment to analogical consistency.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
The practical difference: under Abu Hanifa, if the husband says 'anti taliq kayfa shi'ta' with no intention, one revocable divorce falls immediately. Under Sahibayn, the wife's wish determines the entire outcome (e.g., if she wishes for three, three fall).
This disagreement has direct consequences for fatwa.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : وكيف للسوال عن الحال في أصل وضع اللغة فان استقام فيها والا بطل والا بطل ولذلك قال ابو حنيفة رحمه الله في قوله انت حر كيف شئت انه ايقاع وفي الطلاق تقع الواحدة ويبقى الفضل فى الوصف والقدر مفوضاً اليها بشرط نية الزوج وقالا مالم يقبل الاشارة فحاله ووصفه بمنزلة أصله فيتعلق الاصل بتعلقه ابو حنيفة يقول يلزم من هذا اتباع ا لاصل للوصف وهو خلاف القياس .
اور (کیف) حال کے متعلق سوال کرنے کے لئے آتا ہے جیسے كيف حالك اور اگر سوال کرنے کا معنی صحیح نہ ہو تو کیف کا لفظ باطل ہو جائے گا اس وجہ سے امام صاحب فرماتے ہیں کہ اگر کسی نے اپنے غلام سے کہا انت حر کیف شئت وہ فوری طور پر آزاد ہو جائے گا اور
(كيف) لغو ہو جائے گا ۔ اور انت طالق کیف شیئت کے ذریعہ طلاق دینے کی صورت میں ایک طلاق واقع ہو جائے گی اور وصف اور قدر میں زیادتی عورت کے حوالے ہوگی لیکن اس میں شوہر کی نیت شرط ہے وصف کا مطلب ہے بائنہ ہونا اور قدر کا مطلب ہے کہ تین یا دو طلاقوں کا ہونا اگر میاں بیوی کی نیت متفق ہو تو ان کی نیت کے مطابق طلاق واقع ہو جائے گی اور اگر نیت مختلف ہو تو تعارض کی وجہ سے دونوں نہیں ساقط ہو جائینگی ۔ کیونکہ مشہور قاعدہ ہے اذاتی رنسا تساقطا ۔ اور اصل طلاق

صاحبین فرماتے ہیں کہ ہر وہ چیز جو امور شرعیہ غیر محسوسہ میں سے ہو جیسے طلاق اور عتاق تو اس میں حال اور اصل ایک جیسے ہیں لہذا حال اور اصل دونوں شئت سے متعلق ہونگے تا کہ ترجیح
بلا مرجح لازم نہ آئے۔
جواب ... امام ابوحیفہ قر باتے ہیں کہ اس سے اصل کو وصف کے تابع کر نالا زم آتا ہے اور یہ
خلاف قیاس ہے۔
He said: 'And kayfa is for asking about state in the original language. If it is consistent then [it is valid], otherwise it is invalid. That is why Abu Hanifa said in his statement 'You are free how you wish' that it is an immediate manumission. In divorce, one divorce occurs and the remainder in description and number is left to her conditional upon the husband's intention. The two companions said: as long as it does not accept indication, its state and description are like its original, so the original is attached to its attachment. Abu Hanifa says: from this follows making the original follow the description, which is contrary to analogy.

And (kayfa) comes for asking about state, like 'how are you', and if the meaning of asking is not correct, then the word kayfa becomes invalid. For this reason Imam Abu Hanifa said that if someone said to his slave 'You are free how you wish', he becomes free immediately and (kayfa) becomes لغو. And in the case of saying 'You are divorced how you wish', one divorce occurs and the excess in description and number is left to the wife, but with the condition of the husband's intention. The meaning of description is being irrevocable, and the meaning of number is two or three divorces. If the couple's intentions agree, the divorce occurs accordingly. If intentions differ, due to conflict both intentions fall away, because the famous rule is 'when they conflict, both fall' [اذا تعارضا تساقطا]. And the original divorce is revocable.

The two companions said: Every matter which is among non-sensible legal matters such as divorce and manumission, in them the state and the original are alike, so both the state and the original will relate to the wish in order that preference without preponderance does not occur.

Response: Abu Hanifa says that this entails making the original follow the description, and this is contrary to analogy.
Source pages p. 207

Commentary
This passage presents a detailed dispute regarding the interpretation of 'kayfa' (how) in pronouncements of divorce and manumission. Abu Hanifa holds that 'kayfa' is essentially لغو (redundant) in such contexts, leading to immediate effect: in manumission, the slave is freed immediately; in divorce, one revocable divorce occurs immediately, while the wife is given the option to choose the type (revocable or irrevocable) and number (two or three) of divorces, but this option is subject to the husband's intention. If the husband has no intention, the divorce remains one revocable. The two companions (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani) argue that in non-sensible legal matters like divorce, the state and description are equivalent to the original, so both should be linked to the wife's wish. Abu Hanifa responds that this would make the original (the actual divorce) dependent on the description, which is contrary to qiyas (analogy). The passage also mentions the rule 'when they conflict, both fall' (اذا تعارضا تساقطا) regarding conflicting intentions.

Key Terms
كيف (kayfa): Particle asking about state; here interpreted by Abu Hanifa as لغو in this context.
وصف (wasf): Description, e.g., ba'inah (irrevocable divorce).
قدر (qadr): Number, e.g., two or three divorces.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Abu Hanifa, if a man says to his wife 'Anti taliq kayfa shi'ta' without any intention, what is the ruling?
A) No divorce occurs
B) One revocable divorce occurs and the wife can choose the description and number
C) Three divorces occur immediately
D) The divorce is suspended on the wife's wish only

Key Takeaway
The interpretation of 'kayfa' in talaq and itq illustrates a fundamental usul dispute: whether literal linguistic rules or contextual necessity govern non-sensible legal matters.

The Phrase 'Kam Shi'ta' in Divorce and the Dispute Between Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn
Explaining the ruling on 'anti taliq kamma shi't' and the ikhtilaf about the wife's intention

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi position on the validity of intending two vs three divorces when the husband says 'anti taliq kamma shi't', and explain the usul concepts of adad mahd and fard hukmi that underlie the dispute.

Core Idea
Not all numbers are equal: two is a mere number, three is a hukmi individual — this usul distinction governs the husband's secret intent.

This passage examines a classic usul dispute between Imam Abu Hanifa and his two disciples (the Sahibayn) regarding the effect of a man's unexpressed intention when he says 'you are divorced as much as you wish'. The disagreement hinges on whether the wife's wish (mashi'a) relates only to the act of divorce itself or extends to the number the husband secretly intended. By analyzing this case, you will learn a foundational Hanafi principle: in non-sensual shari' matters, the 'state' (hal) and the 'origin' (asl) must be carefully distinguished, and mere numbers (adad mahd) lack the legal individuality required to be valid objects of intention.

Guided Path
1
Identify the central case and the ikhtilaf
The case is when a husband says 'anti taliq kamma shi't' (you are divorced as much as you wish). The ikhtilaf is whether the husband's secret intention of a specific number (two or three) is effective in determining the number of divorces that occur when the wife wishes.
2
Define the Hanafi concepts: adad mahd and fard hukmi
Understand that in Hanafi usul, some numbers like two are considered 'mere numbers' (adad mahd) with no legal individuality, while three is treated as a 'hukmi individual' because it completes the maximum permissible divorces and thus carries legal significance.
Adad mahd: a number that does not correspond to a real or hukmi individual. Fard hukmi: a legally recognized individual that is a single unit, e.g., three divorces.
3
Apply the concepts to the case
Using Abu Hanifa's position: intending two divorces is invalid because two is adad mahd and not a valid object of intent; intending three is valid because three is fard hukmi. In the absence of a valid intention, only one divorce occurs as the default (since the act itself is one divorce).
4
Analyze the Sahibayn's objection
The Sahibayn argue that in non-sensual shari' matters like divorce, the current state (hal) and the original intent (asl) are the same; both must be linked to the wife's wish to avoid arbitrary preference (tarjih bila murajjih). Therefore, the husband's intended number should also be tied to the wife's wish, making both two and three valid if intended.
5
Evaluate Abu Hanifa's response
Imam Abu Hanifa responds that the Sahibayn's reasoning would make the origin (asl) subordinate to the description (wasf), which is against qiyas. Thus, the default remains that the wife's wish relates only to the act, not the number. The practical result: if the husband intended two, only one divorce occurs; if he intended three, three occur.
This step shows the usul principle that in ambiguous expressions, the origin (asl) takes precedence over the description.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
رجعی واقع ہو جائے گی اس صورت میں دو طلاق کی نیت صحیح نہیں ہے کیونکہ وہ عدد محض ہے کیونکہ یہ نہ فرد حقیقی ہے نہ حکمی اور تین کی نیت صحیح ہے اس لئے کہ وہ فرد حکمی ہے۔ صاحبین فرماتے ہیں کہ ہر وہ چیز جو امور شرعیہ غیر محسوسہ میں سے ہو جیسے طلاق اور عتاق تو اس میں حال اور اصل ایک جیسے ہیں لہذا حال اور اصل دونوں شئت سے متعلق ہونگے تا کہ ترجیح بلا مرجح لازم نہ آئے۔ جواب ... امام ابو حنیفہ فرماتے ہیں کہ اس سے اصل کو وصف کے تابع کرنا لازما آتا ہے اور یہ خلاف قیاس ہے۔
The divorce will be raj'i. In this case, intending two divorces is not valid because it is a mere number (adad mahd), not a real individual nor a hukmi individual. Intending three is valid because it is a hukmi individual. The two companions (Sahibayn) say: Every matter that is among non-sensual shari' matters, like divorce and manumission, in it the state (hal) and the origin (asl) are the same. Therefore both the state and the origin will be related to the wish (shi't) so that preference without a preponderant does not become necessary. The response: Imam Abu Hanifa says that this necessitates making the origin subordinate to the description, and this is against qiyas.
Source pages p. 208

Commentary
This passage discusses a specific case in divorce law. When a man says 'anti taliq kamma shi't' (you are divorced as much as you wish), the ruling according to the Hanafi school is that the divorce is not effective until the wife actually wishes it. The passage then addresses a nuanced issue: if the man intended a specific number (two or three) while saying this, what is the validity? According to the text, intending two divorces is not valid because two is a 'mere number' (adad mahd) and does not constitute a real or hukmi individual. However, intending three is valid because three is considered a 'hukmi individual' (fard hukmi) in the sense that it completes the maximum permissible divorces. The Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani) disagree, arguing that in non-sensual shari' matters like divorce, the current state (hal) and the original intent (asl) must be treated equally to avoid arbitrary preference. They say both should be linked to the wife's wish. Abu Hanifa responds that this reasoning makes the original intent subordinate to the description, which is contrary to qiyas (analogical reasoning). This is a classic Hanafi usul debate about the interaction between the literal wording and the underlying intent.

Key Terms
عدد محض (adad mahd): A mere number that does not correspond to a real or hukmi individual.
فرد حکمی (fard hukmi): A legally recognized individual that is treated as a single unit, such as three divorces.
حال (hal): The current state or context of the speech.
اصل (asl): The origin or default ruling; the underlying legal basis.
وصف (wasf): A description or attribute; here, the number of divorces.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Imam Abu Hanifa, if a man says 'anti taliq kamma shi't' and intends two divorces, what is the ruling?
A) Two divorces occur, one raj'i and one bain
B) One raj'i divorce occurs
C) No divorce occurs
D) Three divorces occur

Key Takeaway
In ambiguous divorce expressions, only numbers that are legal individuals (fard hukmi) are valid objects of intent; mere numbers (adad mahd) are ignored.

Kinayah: Implicit Words and Their Legal Effect
Defining kinayah and applying the rule that it does not establish hudud

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify kinayah words in Hanafi legal texts, apply the rule that kinayah requires intention for legal effect, and explain why hudud are not established by kinayah.

Core Idea
Kinayah: hidden meaning; intention reveals the ruling, but doubt repels hudud.

In Hanafi usul, not all words are created equal. Some are sarih (explicit) and require no intention; others are kinayah (implicit) and their legal effect depends on the speaker's intent or context. This lesson examines the definition of kinayah, its application in divorce, and the critical principle that hudud are never established through kinayah due to the doctrine of repelling punishments by doubts. We will also resolve a debated issue about the effect of grammatical inflection on the word 'anti wahidah'.

Guided Path
1
Distinguish sarih from kinayah
Sarih words have a single clear meaning; kinayah words have multiple possible meanings. The speaker's intention or context determines which meaning is legally operative.
In Hanafi law, intention is required for kinayah to produce a legal effect.
2
Analyze divorce-related kinayah
Words like i'taddi, istabri'i rahimak, and anti wahidah are kinayah because they can refer to non-divorce meanings. However, Hanafi jurists hold that they implicitly contain the word talaq (divorce), making them close to sarih.
Thus, when the husband intends divorce, a raj'i (revocable) divorce occurs.
3
Apply the hudud rule
Since kinayah involves ambiguity (shubha), it cannot establish a hadd punishment. The maxim 'al-hudud tudra'u bi al-shubuhat' applies.
Examples: saying 'I had intercourse' without admission of zina; or responding 'sadaqta' to an accusation.
4
Resolve the i'rab dispute
Some scholars claim that 'anti wahidah' with raf' (nominative) does not cause divorce, but Hanafis rule that i'rab is irrelevant. The correct view is that both raf' and nasb produce the same ruling.
The decisive factor is the husband's intention, not the grammatical case.

Source Text

ليكن يه معلوم نهيں كه كس چيز سے جدا ہے شوہر سے، خاندان سے، مال سے يا جمال سے تو جب شوہر نے نيت كر لي كه يه مجھ سے جدا ہے تو طلاق بائن واقع ہو جائے گي۔
كنايہ وه ہوتا ہے جس كا لغوي معني معلوم ہو ليكن معني مرادي پوشيدہ ہو اور يه الفاظ بهي ايسے ہيں لہذا يه كنايہ ہونے چاہيئں اس لئے بعض لوگوں نے كہا ہے يه علمائے بيان کے مذهب کے مطابق كنايات ہيں۔ اور علمائے اصول کے مذهب کے مطابق يه كنايات ميں سے نهيں ہيں ليكن علمائے بيان کے نزديك كنايہ وه ہوتا ہے كه لفظ كو ذكر كيا جائے اور اس كا معني موضوع لہ مراد ليا جائے ليكن اس كي ذات كي حيثيت سے نهيں بلكه اس حيثيت سے كه لازم معني سے اس کے مردم كي طرف انتقال كيا جائے جيسے طويل النجاد۔
قوله: الا اعتدى واستبرئي رحمك وأنت واحدة ليكن اعتدى، واستبرئي رحمك، وانت واحدة من الفاظ ايسے ہيں كه دائي ہونے کے باوجود ان سے طلاق رجعي واقع ہوتي ہے اس لئے كه ان الفاظ ميں لفظ طلاق تقديرا موجود ہے مثلاً اعتدي ميں الله كي نعمتوں كو شمار كرنے كا بهي احتمال ہے اور عدت سے فارغ ہونے كے لئے حيض كو شمار كرنے كا بهي احتمال ہے جب حيض كي نيت كر لي تو طلاق رجعي واقع ہوگئي گويا كه اس نے مسبب يعني عدت كا ذكر كيا اور مراد اس سے سبب يعني طلاق كي عبارت يه ہے كه انت طالق فاعتدى۔
اسي طرح و استبرئي ميں يه بهي احتمال ہے كه طلب بر اور رحم كر رہا ہو اولاد كے لئے يا دوسرے نكاح كے لئے جب دوسرے نكاح كي نيت كر لي تو طلاق رجعي واقع ہو جائے گي عورت بقدری ہوگي انت طالق فاستبرئي رحمك۔
اب رها انت واحدة اس ميں يه بهي احتمال ہے كه تو مال کے اعتبار سے يهاں کے برها مال اعتبار سے جدا ہے اور يه بهي احتمال ہے انت طالق طلقة واحدة۔ جب طلاق كي نيت كر لي تو طلاق رجعي واقع ہوگي۔ اس لئے بعض كہتے ہيں كه اگر واحدۃ كو رفع كے ساتھ پڑھا جائے تو اس صورت ميں عورت كو كبهي بهي طلاق واقع نهيں ہوگي اور اگر اس كو نصب كے ساتھ پڑھيں گے تو اس صورت ميں عورت پر طلاق واقع ہوگي۔ ليكن صحيح بات يه ہے كه اس ميں اعراب كا كوئي اعتبار نهيں ہے لہذا حكم کے اعتبار سے واحدۃ مرفوع ومنصوب برابر ہيں۔
قوله: والاصل في الكلام الصريح ففى الكناية ضرب قصور ويظهر هذا التفاوت فيما يدرء بالشبهات. اور حكام ميں اصل صريح ہے كيونكه كنايہ ميں كوئي نہ كوئي نہي ہوتا ہے اس لئے كنايت حدود ثابت نهيں ہوتيں چنانچہ اگر كوئي كہے جامعت فلاناً جما عا تو حد ز نالازى نهيں ہوگي، ايسے طرح ايك شخص دوسرے سے سب ہو كر كہے زنيت اور اس نے جواب ميں كہا صدقت تو اس پر حد زنا واجب نهيں ہوں گے كيونكه اس كے اندر اس بات كا بهي احتمال ہے كه تو نے پہلے سچ كہا اور اب جھوٹ كہہ رہا ہے اور اس بات كا بهي احتمال ہے جو تو كہہ رہا ہے وه سچ ہے بخلاف اس صورت كے كه اس نے كسي پر تہمت نكالي زنا كے ساتھ اور دوسرے نے كہا كه وه ايسے ہي ہے جيسے تو نے كہا تو اس صورت ميں حد لگائي جائے گي كيونكه اس كے اس قول هو كما قلت كا مطلب يه ہے كه واقعتا اس نے زنا كيا ہے۔ گويا كه كنايہ يهاں پر باطل ہو گيا اور صريح بن گيا لہذا صريح سے حد قذف واجب ہو جائے گي۔
But it is not known from what it is separated: from husband, family, wealth, or beauty. So when the husband intends that she is separated from me, a ba'in divorce occurs. Kinayah is what has a known lexical meaning but the intended meaning is hidden, and these words are also like that. Therefore some said that according to the scholars of balagha (bayan), they are kinayah, and according to the scholars of usul, they are not from among kinayah. But according to the scholars of balagha, kinayah is when a word is mentioned and its original meaning is intended, but not in terms of its essence, but in terms of transferring from its necessary meaning to its intended meaning, like 'tawil al-nijad' (long-necked). His statement: "except i'taddi, istabri'i rahimak, and anti wahidah" – but i'taddi, istabri'i rahimak, and anti wahidah are words which, despite being ambiguous, cause raj'i divorce because these words implicitly contain the word talaq. For example, in i'taddi, there is also the possibility of counting Allah's blessings, and the possibility of counting menses to complete the waiting period. When he intends menses, a raj'i divorce occurs, as if he mentioned the effect (iddah) and intended the cause (talaq), i.e., "anti taliqun fa'taddi". Similarly, in istabri'i, there is also the possibility that he is requesting purification and mercy for a child or for another marriage. When he intends another marriage, a raj'i divorce occurs; the woman becomes separated, i.e., "anti taliqun fastabri'i rahimak". As for 'anti wahidah', there is also the possibility that she is separate in terms of wealth, or separate in terms of property, and also the possibility that she is 'anti taliqun talqatan wahidah'. When he intends divorce, a raj'i divorce occurs. Therefore some say that if 'wahidah' is read with raf', then in that case divorce never occurs, and if it is read with nasb, then divorce occurs. But the correct view is that inflection (i'rab) is not considered, so in terms of ruling, 'wahidah' with raf' and nasb are equal. His statement: 'The principle is that explicit words (sarih) are the foundation, so kinayah has a type of deficiency. This difference appears in what is repelled by doubts.' The principle is that explicit words are the foundation, because kinayah involves some deficiency (ambiguity). Therefore, hudud are not established by kinayah. For example, if someone says 'I had intercourse with so-and-so', then the hadd for zina is not imposed. Similarly, if a person reviles another saying 'You committed zina' and the other responds 'You said the truth', then the hadd for zina is not obligatory because there is the possibility that he meant 'you spoke truthfully earlier and now you are lying', and also the possibility that what you are saying is true. Unlike the case where someone accuses another of zina and the other says 'it is as you said', then the hadd (for qadhf) is imposed because his statement 'huwa kama qulta' means that indeed he committed zina. So here, the kinayah becomes invalid and becomes explicit (sarih), so the hadd for qadhf becomes obligatory.
Source pages p. 212

Commentary
This passage discusses the legal nature of kinayah (implicit speech) in Hanafi fiqh. The author clarifies that kinayah is a word whose lexical meaning is known but the intended meaning is not directly stated, requiring evidence or intention. In the context of divorce, certain words like 'i'taddi' (observe your waiting period) or 'anti wahidah' (you are one) are kinayah; they do not produce a divorce unless the husband intends divorce. However, these specific words are considered close to explicit (sarih) because they implicitly contain the word 'talaq' in their structure, so they result in a raj'i (revocable) divorce when intended. The passage then discusses the rule that in matters of hadd (fixed punishments), kinayah does not establish a hadd due to the principle of 'daf' al-hudud bi al-shubuhat' (repelling hudud by doubts). For example, saying 'I had intercourse with someone' is kinayah and does not trigger the hadd of zina. Similarly, a confession of zina made in response to a taunt is kinayah. Only when the response is clearly an admission (like 'huwa kama qulta') does the hadd apply. The author also addresses a dispute about the word 'wahidah' with different grammatical endings, stating that the ruling does not depend on i'rab.

Key Terms
صريح (sarih): A word that is clearly understood to have only one meaning, with no ambiguity.
كناية (kinayah): A word whose lexical meaning is known but the intended meaning is hidden and requires context or intention.
رجعة (raj'a): Revocable divorce; the husband can take back his wife during the waiting period without a new marriage.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If a husband says to his wife 'i'taddi' (observe your iddah) intending divorce, what type of divorce occurs according to Hanafi fiqh?
A) No divorce occurs because it is kinayah without explicit mention.
B) Ba'in divorce occurs because it implies separation.
C) Raj'i (revocable) divorce occurs because the word implicitly contains 'talaq'.
D) Talaq al-bid'ah occurs because it is a kinayah used in a single session.

Key Takeaway
Kinayah is a fundamental concept in Hanafi usul: it requires intention for legal effect, and its ambiguity repels hudud but not civil obligations like divorce.

The Principle of Sarih vs Kinayah in Legal Presumptions
Applying the usul principle that clear speech is default and kinayah has deficiency affecting legal consequences

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi principle that sarih speech establishes legal effects without intent, while kinayah requires intent and cannot establish hudud penalties.

Core Idea
Sarih speech is legally binding without intent; kinayah is deficient and cannot trigger hudud.

This lesson explores a core usul principle: speech is presumed explicit (sarih) unless shown to be allusive (kinayah). Kinayah carries a deficiency that limits its legal force, particularly in hudud where doubts repel punishment. The source also addresses a related dispute about the effect of i‘rab in divorce expressions, affirming that the vowel ending of ‘wahidah’ does not alter the ruling.

Guided Path
1
Understand the i‘rab dispute
Identify the ikhtilaf about the vowel ending of 'wahidah' in the phrase 'anti taliq wahidah'. Some held that rafa‘ prevents divorce, while nasb effects it.
This is an intra-Hanafi difference.
2
Recognize the correct Hanafi position
The correct view is that i‘rab is legally irrelevant; both rafa‘ and nasb are valid expressions of divorce, resulting in a revocable divorce.
3
State the general usul principle
The default in speech is sarih (explicit). Kinayah contains a deficiency, leading to restricted legal consequences.
This principle is applied across fiqh, especially in hudud.
4
Apply the principle to hudud
Kinayah cannot establish hudud penalties because hudud are repelled by doubts. Examples: saying 'I had intercourse' without confession does not trigger hadd; affirming 'sadaqta' to a confession does not confirm zina due to doubt.
5
Distinguish the qadhf example
If someone accuses another of zina and the accused says 'it is as you said', this is kinayah that becomes sarih through context, thus establishing hadd qadhf.
The key is context: the accused's statement confirms the accusation, removing doubt.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
اعتبار سے جدا ہے اور یہ بھی احتمال ہے انت طالق طلقة واحدة کرلی تو طلاق رجعی واقع ہوگی۔ اس لئے بعض کہتے ہیں کہ اگر واحدۃ کو رفع کے ساتھ پڑھا جائے تو اس صورت میں عورت کو کبھی بھی طلاق واقع نہیں ہوگی اور اگر اس کو نصب کے ساتھ پڑھیں گے تو اس صورت میں عورت پر طلاق واقع ہوگی۔ لیکن صحیح بات یہ ہے کہ اس میں اعراب کا کوئی اعتبار نہیں ہے لہذا حکم کے اعتبار سے واحدۃ مرفوع ومنصوب برابر ہیں۔ قوله : والاصل في الكلام الصريح ففى الكناية ضرب قصور ويظهر هذا التفاوت فيما يدرء بالشبهات. اور حکام میں اصل صریح ہے کیونکہ کنایہ میں کوئی نہ کوئی نہ تھی ہوتا ہے اسلئے کنایت حدود ثابت نہیں ہوتیں چنانچہ اگر کوئی کہے جامعت فلاناً جماعاً تو حد زنا لازمی نہیں ہوگی، اسی طرح ایک شخص دوسرے سے کہے زَنَيْتُ اور اس نے جواب میں کہا صدقت تو اس پر حد زنا واجب نہیں ہوں کیونکہ اس کے اندر اس بات کا بھی احتمال ہے کہ تو نے پہلے سچ کہا اور اب جھوٹ کہہ رہا ہے اور اس بات کا بھی احتمال ہے جو تو کہہ رہا ہے وہ بچ ہے بخلاف اس صورت کے کہ اس نے کسی پر تہمت نکالی زنا کے ساتھ اور دوسرے نے کہا کہ وہ اسی طرح ہے جیسے تو نے کہا تو اس صورت میں مد لگائی جائے کیونکہ اس کے اس قول هو كما قلت کا مطلب یہ ہے کہ واقع اس نے زنا کیا ہے۔ گویا کہ کنایہ یہاں پر باطل ہو گیا اور صریح بن گیا لہذا صریح سے حد قذف واجب ہو۔
It is separate from consideration and there is also probability that you are divorced with one divorce [if said] so revocable divorce occurs. Therefore some say that if 'واحدة' is read with rafa', then in that case the woman will never be divorced, and if it is read with nasb, then in that case divorce will occur. But the correct view is that i'rab has no consideration in this; thus according to the ruling, marfu' and mansub 'واحدة' are equal. His saying: 'And the default in speech is sarih (explicit), while in kinayah there is a type of deficiency; and this difference appears in what is repelled by doubts.' And in rulings the default is sarih because in kinayah there is some obscurity, therefore by kinayah hudud are not established. For example, if someone says 'I had intercourse with so-and-so' then the hadd of zina will not be necessary. Similarly, one person says to another 'I committed zina' and he responds 'you told the truth' (sadaqta), then the hadd of zina is not obligatory on him because there is also the probability that you told the truth earlier and now you are lying, and also the probability that what you are saying is false. Contrary to the case where someone accuses another of zina and the other says 'it is as you said', then in that case qadhf is applied because his statement 'it is as you said' means that indeed he committed zina. So the kinayah here becomes invalid and becomes sarih, therefore from sarih the hadd of qadhf becomes obligatory.
Source pages p. 213

Commentary
This passage discusses a usul principle regarding the default in speech and its application to legal consequences. The author first addresses a specific issue in divorce: whether the vowel ending (i'rab) of 'wahidah' affects the occurrence of divorce. Some opine that if it is read with rafa' (nominative), no divorce occurs, but with nasb (accusative), it does. However, the correct Hanafi position is that the i'rab is irrelevant; both are considered equally as a valid expression of divorce, resulting in a revocable divorce (talaq raj'i). Then the principle is stated: the default in speech is sarih (explicit), while kinayah (allusive speech) contains a deficiency. This deficiency prevents the application of severe penalties like hudud because doubts repel punishments (dar' al-hudud bi al-shubuhat). Two examples are given: (1) If someone says 'I had intercourse with so-and-so' (without confession in a legal setting), the hadd of zina is not applied because it may be kinayah. (2) If one says 'I committed zina' and another affirms 'you told the truth', the hadd is not applied because the affirmation may be regarding the truthfulness of the statement as a past act, not necessarily confirming actual zina (there is doubt). However, if someone accuses another of zina (qadhf) and the accused says 'it is as you said', then this is considered a confirmation of the accusation, making the kinayah effectively sarih, and the hadd of qadhf becomes obligatory. This illustrates how the principle of default and doubt operates.

Key Terms
صریح (sarih): Clear and explicit expression that directly conveys the intended meaning without ambiguity.
کنایہ (kinayah): An expression that is ambiguous and can bear multiple meanings; not sufficient for establishing hadd penalties.
حدود (hudud): Fixed corporal punishments for crimes like zina, qadhf, theft, etc.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Because the second person is not the one who committed zina
B) Because there is doubt that the second person might be lying or the first might be lying
C) Because qadhf requires four witnesses
D) Because the statement 'sadaqta' is not explicit

Key Takeaway
The default in speech is sarih; kinayah is deficient and cannot establish hudud because doubts repel punishments.

Ikhtilaf — Scholarly Disagreement

Imam Shafi'i's Two Evidential Principles on Conditions and Descriptions
Presenting Imam Shafi'i's two evidential principles with examples and branches

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify Imam Shafi'i's two usul principles regarding conditions and descriptions, apply them to derive rulings, and compare the Hanafi position.

Core Idea
Imam al-Shafi'i treats descriptions like conditions; Hanafis restrict only explicit conditions from restricting rulings.

This passage presents Imam Shafi'i's two main evidential principles for treating descriptive attributes (wasf) as legally operative like conditions (shart). The author, a Hanafi scholar, reviews these principles with Quranic and analogical examples, then draws branch rulings. Understanding this dispute is key to grasping how the two schools differ in interpreting the scope of Quranic and Hadith texts.

Guided Path
1
Identify the first Shafi'i principle: description annexed to condition (الحاق الوصف بالشرط)
The passage explains that Shafi'i equates a description (wasf) with a condition (shart) in tying a ruling to a specific attribute. Note the example: 'You are divorced while riding' is treated like 'if you are riding'.
This principle allows Shafi'i to restrict rulings based on adjectives.
2
Identify the second principle: suspension by condition prevents the ruling but not the cause (تعلیق بالشرط امنع فی الحکم لا فی السبب)
The passage uses the lantern analogy: hanging prevents falling (ruling) but not heaviness (cause). Similarly, conditional divorce (if you enter, then you are divorced) prevents the divorce ruling but not the cause (the statement 'you are divorced').
The cause remains present even if the condition is unfulfilled.
3
Examine the first branch issue: invalid suspension of divorce/manumission upon future ownership
Shafi'i says if a man says to a stranger 'If I marry you, then you are divorced' before marriage, it is invalid because the condition (marriage) is attached to a non-existent basis; even after marriage, no divorce occurs.
The suspension is void because the condition is impossible at the time of utterance.
4
Examine the second branch issue: expiation by wealth before breaking an oath
The passage begins to discuss Shafi'i's view that one may expiate before breaking an oath, contrasting with the Hanafi position that expiation is only after the oath is broken.
This branch applies the same logic: the condition (breaking the oath) is suspended but the cause (the oath) exists.
5
Compare with the Hanafi position
Hanafis reject both principles: descriptions do not condition rulings, and conditions can prevent the cause as well if impossible. The Quranic example (4:25) shows only the explicit condition (inability to afford free woman) restricts, not the description 'believing'.
The thamarah al-khilaf appears in permissibility of marrying non-believing slave women.

Source Text

کی شرط کے ساتھ مشروط کیا ہے۔ لہذا جہاں یہ شرط نہ پائی جائے یعنی جو شخص مومن آزاد عورت سے نکاح کرنے پر قدرت رکھتا ہو اس کے لئے باندیوں سے نکاح جائز نہیں (۲) دوسری شق کی مثال : آیت میں ای آیت میں اللہ ہوگا۔ وحاصله الأول انه الحق بالشرط و الثاني : منبر الخ. ماتن رحمتہ اللہ نے امام شافعی کی دلیل کا جائزہ لیتے ہوئے فرمایا کہ امام شافعی کی دلیل کی بنیاد دو چیزوں پر ہے جن کا بیان مندرجہ ذیل ہے (۱) وصف الغرض کسی نے اپنی بیوی کو یوں طلاق دی انت طالق راكبة تو ان کے ہاں طلاق اس کے رکوب کے ساتھ معلق ہوگی جس طرح اس قول میں ہے انت طالق ان كنت راكبة ، راكبة حال ہے اور قائم بالغیر ہونے کی بناء پر وصف بھی ہے رہے گا مثلاً ان دخلت الدار فأنت طالق کی صورت میں حکم ( وقوع طلاق) کے لئے تو تعلیق ( دخول دار ) مانع ہے لیکن سبب میں مانع نہیں جو انت طالق ہے اور حساً موجود ہیگا۔ الغرض دخول دار نہ پایا جائے تو طلاق کا واقع نہ ہو نا عدم شرعی ہو گا۔ اور امام شافعی کے مذھب کی مزید تنقیح اس حسی مثال سے ہوسکتی ہے۔ اگر کوئی آدمی مثلاً لالٹین کو رسی یا تار وغیرہ سے لٹکا دے تو یہ عمل اس کے حکم ( گرنے) سے مانع ہے لیکن سبب حکم ( بھاری پن ) سے مانع نہیں ، کیونکہ لٹکانے کے باوجود اس کا بھاری پن برقرار ہے مذهب شوافع کی مزید وضاحت کیلئے تفریعات: (۱) پہلا مسئلہ حتى ابطل تعليق الطلاق والعتاق بالملک: امام شافعی فرماتے ہیں کہ مالک بن جائے تو بھی اس کلام کی وجہ سے نہ طلاق واقع ہوگی اور نہ ہی غلام آزاد ہو گا۔ (۲) دوسرا مسئله وجوزا لتكفير بالمال قبل الحنث : - كنارہ مالیہ کے متعلق امام شافعی کا مذھب یہ
It will come. (1) Example of the first clause: Allah Most High said: 'And whoever among you cannot afford to marry free believing women, then from what your right hands possess...' (Quran). Method of derivation: Allah Most High made marriage to slave women conditional upon the absence of means for a free [woman]. Therefore, wherever this condition is not found—i.e., a person who has the ability to marry a free believing woman—marriage to slave women is not permissible for him. (2) Example of the second clause: In the verse, Allah Most High described slave women with the attribute of 'believing': He said: '...then from what your right hands possess of your believing slave girls.' Hence, the description indicates the negation of the ruling for what is other [than described], i.e., marriage to slave women from the People of the Book is not permissible. The summary: The first [clause] indicates the attachment of the ruling to a condition, and the second: minbar etc. The author (may Allah have mercy on him) said, while reviewing the evidence of Imam Shafi'i, that Imam Shafi'i's evidence is based on two things, the explanation of which is as follows: (1) Annexing the description to the condition: Imam Shafi'i opines that just as the existence of the ruling depends on the existence of the condition and its non-existence on the non-existence of the condition (if the condition exists, the ruling exists; otherwise not), similarly is the case with a description. For instance, if a man divorces his wife by saying 'You are divorced while riding,' then in their [Shafi'i] view, the divorce is suspended upon her riding, just as in the statement 'You are divorced if you are riding.' 'Riding' is a circumstantial accusative and, due to its being subsisting by another, it is also a description. (2) Suspension by condition is a preventer in the ruling, not in the cause: Imam Shafi'i says that if a ruling is suspended upon a condition, this suspension prevents the ruling up to the existence of the condition, but its cause remains. For example, in the case of 'If you enter the house, then you are divorced,' the suspension (entry into the house) prevents the ruling (occurrence of divorce), but it does not prevent the cause, which is the statement 'you are divorced' which exists sensibly. Thus, if entry into the house does not occur, the non-occurrence of divorce is a legal non-existence. Further clarification of Imam Shafi'i's school can be had from this sensory example: If a person, for instance, hangs a lantern with a rope or wire, this action prevents its ruling (falling) but does not prevent the cause of the ruling (heaviness), because despite hanging, its heaviness remains. For further clarification of the Shafi'i school, [here are] branch issues: (1) First issue: He invalidated suspension of divorce and manumission upon ownership: Imam Shafi'i says that if a person says to a strange woman 'If I marry you, then you are divorced' (2) Or if someone says to a slave 'If I own you, then you are free,' or 'If I own you, then you are free'—then this statement is invalid because the suspension does not prevent the existence of the cause. Therefore, respectively, the causes 'you are divorced' and 'you are free' exist and are in need of [؟], but since the basis, i.e., marriage and ownership, is absent, this statement is invalid. If later this person marries the strange woman or becomes owner of the slave, still neither divorce will occur nor will the slave be freed due to this statement. (2) Second issue: He permitted expiation with wealth before breaking the oath: Regarding monetary expiation, Imam Shafi'i's school is that...
Source pages p. 239

Commentary
This passage is a usul dispute presenting Imam Shafi'i's two principal evidences (دلیل) for treating descriptive attributes (وصف) like conditions (شرط) in legal rulings, followed by two branch rulings (تفریعات). The author, a Hanafi scholar, reviews Shafi'i's arguments. The first evidence is 'annexing the description to the condition' (الحاق الوصف بالشرط): Shafi'i argues that just as a condition makes the ruling contingent on its existence, a description does the same. The example given is divorce pronounced as 'You are divorced while riding' (انت طالق راكبة), which Shafi'is treat as conditional upon riding, like 'if you are riding'. The second evidence is 'suspension by condition prevents the ruling, not the cause' (تعلیق بالشرط امنع فی الحکم لا فی السبب): using the analogy of hanging a lantern—the hanging prevents falling (ruling) but not heaviness (cause). Similarly, in conditional divorce (if you enter, then you are divorced), the condition (entry) prevents the ruling (divorce) but not the cause (the statement of divorce). The branch issues apply these principles: First, if someone says to a stranger 'If I marry you, then you are divorced' before marriage, the statement is invalid; even after marriage, no divorce occurs. Second, Shafi'i allows expiation by wealth before breaking an oath, a view that contrasts with the Hanafi position that expiation is only after the oath is broken. The Hanafi critique, though implicit, is that descriptions do not condition rulings in the same way as explicit conditions; the Quranic examples presented earlier (conditions for marrying slave women) show that only explicit conditions restrict permissibility.

Key Terms
شرط (shart): A condition that makes the existence of a ruling dependent upon its own existence; if it is absent, the ruling is absent.
وصف (wasf): A descriptive attribute that qualifies the subject of a ruling, e.g., 'believing' in 'believing slave girls'.
تعلیق (ta'liq): Suspending a ruling upon the occurrence of a future condition.
سبب (sabab): The cause or basis of a ruling, e.g., the statement 'you are divorced' is the cause of divorce.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the example used to illustrate Shafi'i's principle that suspension by condition prevents the ruling but not the cause?
A) A lantern hanging from a rope.
B) The Quranic prohibition of marrying polytheist slave girls.
C) The statement 'You are divorced while riding'.
D) Expiating an oath before breaking it.

Key Takeaway
Imam Shafi'i's two principles—descriptions as conditions and suspension not preventing cause—lead to significant differences from Hanafi rulings in family law and oaths.

Ikhtilaf — Scholarly Disagreement
intermediate
8 min
Shafi'i View on Ta'liq: Prevents Ruling Not Cause
Explaining Shafi'i position that conditional suspension blocks the ruling but not the cause

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to differentiate between the Shafi'i and Hanafi positions on ta'liq bi al-shart and apply the Shafi'i rule to conditional statements in usul analysis.

Core Idea
In ta'liq, the condition blocks the occurrence of the ruling but the cause remains effective.

This passage presents the Shafi'i perspective on the nature of conditional suspension (ta'liq bi al-shart). According to Imam al-Shafi'i, when a ruling is suspended upon a condition, only the actualization of the ruling is blocked, not the existence of its cause. This difference with the Hanafi view has practical consequences in cases like conditional divorce and expiation before oath violation. Understanding this ikhtilaf is crucial for analyzing how different schools derive rulings from conditional language.

Guided Path
1
Distinguish sabab and hukm
Identify the cause (sabab) and ruling (hukm) in a conditional statement like 'If you enter the house, you are divorced.' The sabab is the utterance 'you are divorced'; the hukm is the occurrence of divorce.
The Shafi'i hold the sabab exists independently of the condition.
2
Explain the Shafi'i principle
Understand that ta'liq prevents the hukm from taking effect until the condition is met, but does not negate the sabab. The condition is a man' (preventer) of the hukm, not of the sabab.
The lantern analogy illustrates: hanging prevents falling (hukm) but not heaviness (sabab).
3
Apply to the first furu'
Analyze the case of conditional divorce on future marriage ('If I marry you, you are divorced'). According to Shafi'i, the sabab (the utterance) exists, but marriage is required for the hukm; since marriage is absent, the statement is void.
Even if marriage later occurs, no divorce follows because the ta'liq was invalid from the start.
4
Apply to the second furu'
For expiation (kaffarah) before oath violation, the Shafi'i view permits it because the sabab (the oath) exists. The Hanafi view rejects it because they consider the violation (hanth) as the sabab, not the oath.
Physical expiation is not disputed because there the obligation and payment are simultaneous.
5
Contrast with Hanafi position
The Hanafis hold that ta'liq prevents the sabab itself, so the cause is not constituted until the condition is met. This leads to different rulings on the voidness of certain conditional statements and on expiation timing.
This difference stems from the definition of sabab in conditional utterances.

تعلیق بالشرط امنع حکم میں عامل ہے نہ کہ منع سبب میں: امام شافعی فرماتے ہیں کہ اگر کسی حکم کو کسی شرط کے ساتھ معلق کر دیا جائے تو یہ تعلیق اس حکم سے تو وجود شرط تک مانع ہوگی لیکن اس کا سبب موجود رہے گا مثلاً ان دخلت الدار فأنت طالق کی صورت میں حکم ( وقوع طلاق) کے لئے تو تعلیق ( دخول دار ) مانع ہے لیکن سبب میں مانع نہیں جو انت طالق ہے اور حساً موجود ہیگا۔ الغرض دخول دار نہ پایا جائے تو طلاق کا واقع نہ ہو نا عدم شرعی ہو گا۔ اور امام شافعی کے مذھب کی مزید تنقیح اس حسی مثال سے ہوسکتی ہے۔ اگر کوئی آدمی مثلاً لالٹین کو رسی یا تار وغیرہ سے لٹکا دے تو یہ عمل اس کے حکم ( گرنے) سے مانع ہے لیکن سبب حکم ( بھاری پن ) سے مانع نہیں ، کیونکہ لٹکانے کے باوجود اس کا بھاری پن برقرار ہے مذهب شوافع کی مزید وضاحت کیلئے تفریعات: (۱) پہلا مسئلہ حتى ابطل تعليق الطلاق والعتاق بالملک: امام شافعی فرماتے ہیں کہ اگر کوئی شخص کسی اجنبی عورت سے یوں کہے ان نکحتک فانت طالق - (۲) اگر کوئی کسی غلام سے یوں کہے ان ملکت فانت حر ان ملكتک فانت حرة - تويه كلام باطل ہوگا کیونکہ یتعلیق وجود سبب سے مانع نہیں لہذا علی الترتیب سبب انت طالق اور انت حر احرة موجود ہیں اور سی تیل کے محتاج ہیں لیکن چونکہ حمل یعنی زوجیت اور ملکیت معدوم ہے لہذا یہ کلام باطل ہے اگر بعد میں یہ شخص بالترتیب اجنبیہ سے نکاح کر لے یا غلام کا مالک بن جائے تو بھی اس کلام کی وجہ سے نہ طلاق واقع ہوگی اور نہ ہی غلام آزاد ہو گا۔ (۲) دوسرا مسئله وجوزا لتكفير بالمال قبل الحنث : - كنارہ مالیہ کے متعلق امام شافعی کا مذھب یہ ہے کہ اگر کوئی شخص قسم کھانے کے بعد حانث ہونے سے قبل بھی کفارہ ادا کرے تو ادا ہو جائے گا۔ سے قبل بھی کفار وادا چاہے بعد میں حانث ہو جائے۔ اعادہ کی ضرورت نہیں۔ کیونکہ سبب کفارہ یعنی یمین پایا گیا۔ اور سب کی وجہ سے نفس وجوب کفارہ ثابت ہے اگر چہ وجوب ادا و حسٹ کے بعد ہی متحقق ہو گا۔ ادا ء احناف کے ہاں حث سے قبل کفارہ جائز نہیں۔ جس کی تفصیل یہ ہے:۔ احناف کہتے ہیں کہ یہ اداء قبل وجود السبب ہے، جو معتبر نہیں جس طرح وقت سے پہلے نماز ادا کرنا ۔ مبنائے اختلاف سبب میں ہے جو شوافع کے ہاں یمین ہے لیکن احناف کے ہاں حث ہے اور یمین سبب براءت ۔ یاد رہے کہ کفارہ بدنیہ میں کوئی اختلاف نہیں کیونکہ اس میں نفس وجوب سے وجوب ادا منفک نہیں بلکہ دونوں ساتھ پائے جاتے ہیں۔ رہا کفارہ مالیہ تو احناف کے ہاں اس میں بھی کوئی وجہ فرق نہیں کیونکہ مال مقصور نہیں بلکہ حق اللہ میں مقصود اسکی آدائیگی ہوتی ہے ۔ خواہ کسی طرح بھی ہو ۔ لہذا ہے کفارہ مالیہ میں بھی نفس وجوب وجوب اداء سے جدا نہیں ہوگا ۔
Suspension by condition is operative in preventing the ruling, not in preventing the cause. Imam Shafi'i says: If a ruling is suspended upon a condition, this suspension prevents the ruling until the condition exists, but its cause remains. For example, in the case of 'If you enter the house, you are divorced,' the suspension (entry into the house) prevents the ruling (occurrence of divorce), but it does not prevent the cause, which is 'you are divorced' and it exists in reality. In short, if entry into the house does not occur, the non-occurrence of divorce is a legal non-existence. And further clarification of Imam Shafi'i's madhhab can be made with this sensory example: if a person hangs a lantern with a rope or wire, this action prevents its ruling (falling) but does not prevent the cause of the ruling (heaviness), because despite hanging, its heaviness remains. For further elaboration of the Shafi'i madhhab, subsidiary issues: (1) First Issue: He even nullified the suspension of divorce and emancipation upon ownership. Imam Shafi'i says: If a person says to a foreign woman, 'If I marry you, you are divorced,' or if someone says to a slave, 'If I own you, you are free,' or 'If I own you, you are free,' then this speech is void because the suspension does not prevent the existence of the cause. Therefore, respectively, the cause 'you are divorced' and 'you are free' exist and are in need of connection, but since the connection, i.e., marriage and ownership, is absent, this speech is void. If later this person marries the foreign woman or becomes the owner of the slave, neither divorce will occur nor will the slave be freed. (2) Second Issue: He permits expiation with wealth before violation. Regarding monetary expiation, Imam Shafi'i's madhhab is that if a person after swearing an oath gives expiation before violating, it will be valid. Even if he violates later, no repetition is needed, because the cause of expiation, i.e., the oath, is found, and due to the cause, the very obligation of expiation is established, although the obligation of payment is realized only after violation. According to the Hanafis, expiation before violation is not permissible. Its detail is: The Hanafis say this is payment before the existence of the cause, which is not valid, just like performing prayer before its time. The basis of the difference is the cause: according to the Shafi'is it is the oath, but according to the Hanafis it is the violation, and the oath is the cause of expiation [in a different sense?]. Remember that there is no disagreement in physical expiation because in it the obligation itself is not separated from the obligation of payment; rather, both are found together. As for monetary expiation, according to the Hanafis there is no reason for a difference because wealth is not the intended end, rather the intended end regarding the right of Allah is its payment, in whatever way. Therefore, in monetary expiation as well, the obligation itself will not be separate from the obligation of payment.
Source pages p. 240

Commentary
This passage presents the Shafi'i position on the nature of conditional suspension (ta'liq bi al-shart). The author is explaining that according to Imam al-Shafi'i, when a ruling is suspended on a condition, the condition only prevents the actualization of the ruling, not the existence of its cause. The example given is the conditional divorce statement: 'If you enter the house, you are divorced.' The cause of divorce is the statement 'you are divorced,' which exists as a speech act. The condition (entry) prevents the ruling (divorce taking effect) but does not negate the cause. The lantern analogy illustrates this: hanging the lantern prevents it from falling (the ruling), but the heaviness (the cause) remains. The text then elaborates with two subsidiary issues from Shafi'i fiqh. First, if someone says to a stranger 'If I marry you, you are divorced,' the statement is void because the cause exists but the marriage (the condition) is absent; even if marriage later occurs, no divorce takes effect. Second, regarding expiation (kaffarah) for a broken oath, the Shafi'is permit paying the expiation before violating the oath, because the cause (the oath) already exists. The Hanafi view is then contrasted: they consider the cause to be the violation (hanth), not the oath, so paying before violation is like paying before the cause exists. The text ends by noting that there is no disagreement in physical expiation (like fasting) because obligation and payment are simultaneous; for monetary expiation, the Hanafis maintain the same principle because the payment is what is intended, regardless of the form.

Key Terms
تعلیق بالشرط (ta'liq bi al-shart): Suspending a legal ruling (e.g., divorce, emancipation) upon the occurrence of a future condition.
سبب (sabab): The cause or effective reason that would normally produce a ruling; in conditional statements, the speech act that would be the cause if effective.
حکم (hukm): The ruling or legal consequence (e.g., occurrence of divorce, emancipation, or obligation of expiation).
مانع (mani'): A preventive factor that blocks the effect of a cause or the actualization of a ruling.
حنث (hanth): The violation of an oath by doing the sworn act.
کفارہ (kaffarah): Expiation paid for breaking an oath; can be monetary or physical (fasting).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Shafi'i view presented, what does the condition prevent in a conditional statement like 'If you enter the house, you are divorced'?
A) The cause (sabab) of divorce
B) The ruling (hukm) of divorce from taking effect
C) Both the cause and the ruling
D) Neither the cause nor the ruling

Key Takeaway
The Shafi'i position on ta'liq holds that the condition blocks only the ruling, not the cause, leading to different rulings on expiation before violation and conditional statements on future events.

Ikhtilaf — Scholarly Disagreement

Hanafi View: Ta'liq Prevents Both Cause and Ruling
Presenting the Hanafi position that conditional suspension prevents both cause and ruling, refuting Shafi'i examples

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi position on ta'liq bi al-shart and explain how it differs from the Shafi'i view, applying the classification of divorce as isti'arah.

Core Idea
Conditional suspension in Hanafi usul prevents the cause from being constituted until the condition is met, making the statement valid but suspended.

This lesson explores a key usul dispute between the Hanafi and Shafi'i schools regarding conditional legal statements. The Shafi'is held that such statements become effective causes immediately, with only the ruling suspended. In contrast, the Hanafis argue that the cause itself is not constituted until the condition occurs, based on the nature of divorce and emancipation as metaphorical acts. Understanding this difference clarifies important practical rulings in family law.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Shafi'i position
State that Shafi'is consider the conditional statement as a cause that is effective immediately, but the ruling is suspended until the condition.
2
State the Hanafi position
Explain that for Hanafis, the conditional suspension prevents both the cause and the ruling.
3
Understand the linguistic basis
Recognize that Hanafis classify divorce and emancipation as isti'arat (metaphors), requiring complete suspension.
4
Apply the principle to examples
See how the statement 'If I marry you, you are divorced' is not laghw but valid; upon marriage, divorce takes effect.
5
Contrast with Shafi'i view
Note that for Shafi'is, such a statement before ownership would be laghw; Hanafis avoid this by suspending the cause.

وعندنا المعلق بالشرط لا ينعقد سببا الخ شوافع کا مذھب گزر چکا کہ معلق بالشر یا حقیقتا سبب ہوا کرتا ہے۔ احناف کے ہاں معلق بالشرط هیتا سبب منعقد نہیں ہوتا ۔ اگر چہ صورتاً سبب کا دہم ہو کیونکہ طلاق حقیقتاً اور عتاق و غیرہ استعارات میں سے ہیں لہذا ان میں تعلیق کا کامل ہونا ضروری ہے اور وہ یہی ہے کہ شرط سبب اور حکم دونوں سے مانع ہو۔ لہذا انت طالق ان دخلت الدار کی صورت میں جب تک شرط یعنی دخول دار نہ پائی جائے یوں سمجھا جائے گا کہ اس نے سبب انت طالق کا تکلم ہی نہیں کیا۔ جب ہمارے ہاں تعلیق بالشرط کی صورت میں سبب وجود شرط سے پہلے نہیں پایا جاتا تو وہ مل کا مقتضی بھی نہیں۔ لہذا ان نکحتک فانت طالق اور ان ملکتک فانت حر احرة والی مثالوں میں عاقل بالغ کا کلام لغو اور باطل نہیں ہوگا۔ اور جب بھی شہ و کان ملک پائی جائے سبب عامل ہوگا۔ اور اجتنبیہ کو طلاق اور غلام باندی پر آزادی کا حکم ثابت ہوگا۔
And according to us, the suspended by condition does not constitute a cause etc. The madhhab of the Shafi'is has passed that the suspended by condition effectively becomes a cause in reality. According to the Hanafis, the suspended by condition does not become a cause in the form of [complete] cause, even if seemingly it is an intrusion into the cause. Because divorce in reality, and emancipation and others, are among metaphors. Therefore, perfect suspension is necessary in them, and that is that the condition prevents both the cause and the ruling. Thus, in the case of 'You are divorced, if you enter the house,' as long as the condition, i.e., entry into the house, is not found, it will be understood that he did not utter the cause 'you are divorced' at all. When according to us in the case of suspension by condition, the cause is not found before the existence of the condition, then it does not necessitate the ruling. Therefore, in the examples of 'If I marry you, you are divorced' and 'If I own you, you are free,' the speech of a sane adult is not frivolous and void. And whenever marriage and ownership occur, the cause will be operative, and the ruling of divorce for the foreign woman and freedom for the slave will be established.
Source pages p. 241

Commentary
This passage presents the Hanafi position on ta'liq bi al-shart, directly contrasting with the Shafi'i view. The author states that according to the Hanafis, a conditional suspension does not constitute a cause (sabab) at all until the condition is fulfilled. The reason given is that divorce, emancipation, and similar legal acts are metaphorical in nature (isti'arat), so they require a complete suspension where the condition blocks both the cause and the ruling. Therefore, in the statement 'You are divorced if you enter the house,' before entry occurs, it is as if he never uttered the cause 'you are divorced.' This means the conditional statement is not invalid (laghw) or void; rather it is valid but suspended. Thus, the earlier Shafi'i examples—'If I marry you, you are divorced' and 'If I own you, you are free'—are considered valid in the Hanafi school. When the condition (marriage or ownership) later occurs, the cause becomes operative, and divorce or emancipation takes effect. This resolves the Shafi'i problem of the statement being void: for Hanafis, the statement is a valid ta'liq and will produce its effect upon fulfillment of the condition.

Key Terms
معلق بالشرط (mu'allaq bi al-shart): A legal statement (e.g., divorce, emancipation) that is suspended on a future condition.
سبب منعقد (sabab mun'aqid): A cause that is effectively constituted and able to produce its ruling.
استعارات (isti'arat): Metaphors; legal acts like divorce and emancipation are considered metaphorical (isti'arah) rather than literal actions, requiring complete suspension.
لغو (laghw): Frivolous or void speech that has no legal effect.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi view, what happens when a person says to a foreign woman 'If I marry you, you are divorced' and later marries her?
A) The statement is void and divorce does not occur
B) The divorce occurs immediately upon the statement, regardless of marriage
C) The divorce takes effect upon marriage because the cause becomes operative
D) The statement is valid but requires repeating after marriage

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi view that a conditional suspension prevents both cause and ruling ensures that such statements are valid and operative only upon fulfillment of the condition.

The Cause of Expiation: Hanafi vs Shafi'i on Pre-Breach Kaffarah
Usul dispute on cause of kaffarah

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the Hanafi and Shafi'i positions on performing expiation before breaking an oath and apply the Hanafi rule that the cause (sabab) is the breach (hanth) to determine validity.

Core Idea
Expiation before oath-breaking is invalid in the Hanafi school because the cause is the breach, not the oath.

This lesson explores a classic usul al-fiqh dispute: can kaffarah (expiation) for an oath be performed before the oath is broken? The answer hinges on identifying the sabab (cause) that triggers the obligation. Hanafis and Shafi'is differ on the sabab, leading to opposite rulings. Understanding this difference sharpens your ability to trace legal obligations back to their causes.

Guided Path
1
Identify the disputed ruling
What is the action under dispute? Performing kaffarah for an oath before breaking the oath.
2
State each school's position
Hanafi: not valid. Shafi'i: valid. Locate these in the passage.
3
Identify the sabab according to each school
The core difference: Hanafis say the sabab is hanth (breaking); Shafi'is say the sabab is yamin (the oath itself).
4
Analyze the Hanafi qiyas
The Hanafi argument uses qiyas: performing kaffarah before hanth is like praying before its time – both are before the cause exists.
5
Note the exception for bodily expiation
The passage mentions that in bodily expiation (kaffarah badaniyyah, e.g., fasting), there is no dispute because the obligation to perform is simultaneous with the obligation itself.
For financial expiation (kaffarah maliyyah), Hanafis extend the same principle.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ہے کہ اگر کوئی شخص قسم کھانے کے بعد حانث ہونے سے قبل بھی کفارہ ادا کرے تو ادا ہو جائے گا۔ سے قبل بھی کفار وادا چاہے بعد میں حانث ہو جائے۔ اعادہ کی ضرورت نہیں۔ کیونکہ سبب کفارہ یعنی یمین پایا گیا۔ اور سب کی وجہ سے نفس وجوب کفارہ ثابت ہے اگر چہ وجوب ادا و حسٹ کے بعد ہی متحقق ہو گا۔ ادا ء احناف کے ہاں حث سے قبل کفارہ جائز نہیں۔ جس کی تفصیل یہ ہے:۔ احناف کہتے ہیں کہ یہ اداء قبل وجود السبب ہے، جو معتبر نہیں جس طرح وقت سے پہلے نماز ادا کرنا ۔ مبنائے اختلاف سبب میں ہے جو شوافع کے ہاں یمین ہے لیکن احناف کے ہاں حث ہے اور یمین سبب براءت ۔ یاد رہے کہ کفارہ بدنیہ میں کوئی اختلاف نہیں کیونکہ اس میں نفس وجوب سے وجوب ادا منفک نہیں بلکہ دونوں ساتھ پائے جاتے ہیں۔ رہا کفارہ مالیہ تو احناف کے ہاں اس میں بھی کوئی وجہ فرق نہیں کیونکہ مال مقصور نہیں بلکہ حق اللہ میں مقصود اسکی آدائیگی ہوتی ہے ۔ خواہ کسی طرح بھی ہو ۔ لہذا ہے کفارہ مالیہ میں بھی نفس وجوب وجوب اداء سے جدا نہیں ہوگا ۔
It is that if a person after swearing an oath performs expiation before becoming a violator, the expiation will be valid, even if he becomes a violator later, no repetition is needed because the cause of expiation, i.e. the oath, is found, and due to it the obligation of expiation is established, although the obligation to perform is realized after violation. According to Hanafis, expiation before violation is not permissible. The detail is: Hanafis say this is performance before the existence of the cause, which is not valid, like performing prayer before its time. The basis of difference is the cause: according to Shafi'is it is the oath, but according to Hanafis it is violation, and the oath is the cause of freedom. Remember that in bodily expiation there is no difference because in it the obligation to perform is not separate from the obligation itself, rather both are together. As for financial expiation, according to Hanafis there is also no difference because wealth is not restricted, rather what is intended is its payment for the right of Allah, no matter how. Therefore, in financial expiation, the obligation to perform is also not separate from the obligation itself.
Source pages p. 241

Commentary
This passage discusses a dispute between Hanafis and Shafi'is regarding the validity of performing kaffarah (expiation) before the oath is broken (hanth). The Shafi'i view is that since the oath (yamin) is the cause (sabab) of expiation, performing expiation before hanth is valid once the oath is taken. The Hanafi position is that the cause is actually the hanth (breaking), not the oath itself, so performing expiation before the cause exists is like praying before its time—invalid. The author then notes that in bodily expiations (kaffarah badaniyyah, e.g., fasting), there is no dispute because the obligation to perform and the obligation itself are simultaneous. For financial expiation (kaffarah maliyyah, e.g., feeding or freeing slaves), Hanafis maintain the same principle: the obligation to perform is not separate, so early performance is not allowed. The passage is a classic example of how differences in determining the sabab (cause) lead to different fiqhi rulings.

Key Terms
سبب (sabab): The cause that necessitates expiation; in this context, the event that triggers the obligation.
حانث (hanth): The act of breaking an oath, which triggers expiation.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) The oath itself
B) Breaking the oath
C) The intention to break
D) The passage of time

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school requires that expiation for an oath be performed after breaking the oath because the cause (sabab) is the breach, not the oath itself.

Eighth Invalid Argument: Recognizing Ikhtilaf in Usul al-Fiqh
Brief placeholder noting ikhtilaf on a specific usul point.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define fasad (invalid argument) in usul al-fiqh and state the Hanafi approach to ikhtilaf regarding such arguments.

Core Idea
An argument (fasad) is invalid due to a flaw; Hanafi scholarship records numerous differences to ensure thorough legal evaluation.

The commentator’s brief remark that ‘many differences exist’ regarding the eighth invalid argument alerts the student to a recurring feature in usul texts: acknowledging multiple viewpoints. This lesson unpacks what a fasad is and why ikhtilaf matters in building sound legal reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Understand the term fasad
In Hanafi usul, a fasad is an argument that is invalid due to a defect in its structure or premise. It is crucial to identify such flaws to avoid erroneous istinbat.
2
Recognize the commentator's claim
The commentator notes that many differences (ikhtilafat) exist regarding the eighth fasad. This means scholars disagree on whether it is indeed invalid, or on the exact nature of its flaw.
3
Understand the role of ikhtilaf in usul
Ikhtilaf is legitimate scholarly disagreement. Hanafi usul treats it as a resource: cataloguing different views allows a mujtahid to weigh evidence and apply tarjih (preponderance).
4
Apply the principle of thoroughness
The commentator’s cross-reference shows that a complete usul text must address all relevant differences. When encountering a fasad, you must consult the detailed discussion to ensure no sound counterargument is missed.
5
Prepare for tarjih
After gathering all views on a fasad, the next step is to determine which is strongest using Hanafi tarjih principles (e.g., prefer the dalil with higher thubut or dalalah).
